<p><b>Baptists</b> form a major branch of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> distinguished by baptizing professing believers only (<a href="/wiki/Believer%27s_baptism" title="Believer&#39;s baptism">believer's baptism</a>, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">infant baptism</a>), and doing so by complete <a href="/wiki/Immersion_baptism" title="Immersion baptism">immersion</a> (as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Affusion" title="Affusion">affusion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aspersion" title="Aspersion">aspersion</a>). Baptist churches also generally subscribe to the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrines</a> of <a href="/wiki/Soul_competency" title="Soul competency">soul competency</a> (the responsibility and accountability of every person before God), <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">sola fide</a></i> (salvation by faith alone), <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i> (scripture alone as the rule of faith and practice) and <a href="/wiki/Congregationalist_polity" title="Congregationalist polity">congregationalist church government</a>. Baptists generally recognize two <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_(Christianity)" title="Ordinance (Christianity)">ordinances</a>: baptism and <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">communion</a>.
</p><p>Diverse from their beginning, those identifying as Baptists today differ widely from one another in what they believe, how they worship, their attitudes toward other Christians, and their understanding of what is important in Christian discipleship.<sup id="cite_ref-Shurden_turning_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shurden_turning-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Protestant_branches.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Protestant_branches.svg/220px-Protestant_branches.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Protestant_branches.svg/330px-Protestant_branches.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Protestant_branches.svg/440px-Protestant_branches.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Protestant_branches.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph about <a href="/wiki/Protestant_denomination" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant denomination">Protestant affiliations</a>; <b>Baptists</b> shown in dark red.</div></div></div>
<p>Historians trace the earliest "Baptist" church to 1609 in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> with <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">English Separatist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Smyth_(Baptist_minister)" title="John Smyth (Baptist minister)">John Smyth</a> as its pastor.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In accordance with his reading of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, he rejected baptism of infants and instituted baptism only of believing adults.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Baptist practice spread to England, where the <a href="/wiki/General_Baptists" title="General Baptists">General Baptists</a> considered Christ's atonement to <a href="/wiki/Unlimited_atonement" title="Unlimited atonement">extend to all people</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Particular_Baptists" class="mw-redirect" title="Particular Baptists">Particular Baptists</a> believed that it extended only to <a href="/wiki/Election_(Christianity)" title="Election (Christianity)">the elect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Benedict1848_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benedict1848-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helwys" title="Thomas Helwys">Thomas Helwys</a> formulated a distinctively Baptist request that the <a href="/wiki/Baptists_in_the_history_of_separation_of_church_and_state" title="Baptists in the history of separation of church and state">church and the state be kept separate</a> in matters of law, so that individuals might have freedom of religion. Helwys died in prison as a consequence of the religious conflict with <a href="/wiki/English_dissenters" class="mw-redirect" title="English dissenters">English dissenters</a> under <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">King James I</a>. In 1638, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Williams" title="Roger Williams">Roger Williams</a> established the <a href="/wiki/First_Baptist_Church_in_America" title="First Baptist Church in America">first Baptist congregation in the North American colonies</a>. In the 18th and 19th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/First_Great_Awakening" title="First Great Awakening">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Awakening" title="Second Great Awakening">Second Great Awakening</a> increased church membership in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-EBOself_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBOself-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>   Baptist missionaries have spread their faith to every continent.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Baptist historian Bruce Gourley outlines four main views of Baptist origins:
</p>
<ol><li>the modern scholarly consensus that the movement traces its origin to the 17th century via the <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">English Separatists</a>,</li>
<li>the view that it was an outgrowth of <a href="/wiki/Anabaptism" title="Anabaptism">Anabaptist</a> traditions,</li>
<li>the perpetuity view which assumes that the Baptist <i>faith and practice</i> has existed since the time of Christ, and</li>
<li>the successionist view, or "<a href="/wiki/Baptist_successionism" title="Baptist successionism">Baptist successionism</a>", which argues that Baptist <i>churches</i> actually existed in an unbroken chain since the time of Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_separatist_view">English separatist view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: English separatist view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:174px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John-Smyth.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/John-Smyth.png" decoding="async" width="172" height="211" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="172" data-file-height="211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John-Smyth.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Smyth is believed to have the first church labeled "Baptist" in Amsterdam in 1609</div></div></div>
<p>Modern Baptist churches trace their history to the <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">English Separatist</a> movement in the 1600s, the century after the rise of the original Protestant denominations.<sup id="cite_ref-Brackney_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brackney-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This view of Baptist origins has the most historical support and is the most widely accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-ETS_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETS-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Adherents to this position consider the influence of Anabaptists upon early Baptists to be minimal.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>  It was a time of considerable political and religious turmoil. Both individuals and churches were willing to give up their theological roots if they became convinced that a more biblical "truth" had been discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard2003_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard2003-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2014)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (<a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicans</a>) separated from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>. There were some Christians who were not content with the achievements of the mainstream Protestant Reformation.<sup id="cite_ref-Shurden_turning_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shurden_turning-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Briggs_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> There also were Christians who were disappointed that the Church of England had not made corrections of what some considered to be errors and abuses. Of those most critical of the Church's direction, some chose to stay and try to make constructive changes from within the Anglican Church. They became known as "<a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a>" and are described by Gourley as cousins of the English Separatists. Others decided they must leave the Church because of their dissatisfaction and became known as the Separatists.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians trace the earliest Baptist church back to 1609 in Amsterdam, with <a href="/wiki/John_Smyth_(Baptist_minister)" title="John Smyth (Baptist minister)">John Smyth</a> as its pastor.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>  Three years earlier, while a Fellow of Christ's College, Cambridge, he had broken his ties with the Church of England. Reared in the Church of England, he became "Puritan, English Separatist, and then a Baptist Separatist," and ended his days working with the Mennonites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard200323_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard200323-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He began meeting in England with 60–70 English Separatists, in the face of "great danger."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The persecution of religious nonconformists in England led Smyth to go into exile in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> with fellow Separatists from the congregation he had  gathered in Lincolnshire, separate from the established church (Anglican). Smyth and his lay supporter, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helwys" title="Thomas Helwys">Thomas Helwys</a>, together with those they led, broke with the other English exiles because Smyth and Helwys were convinced they should be baptized as believers. In 1609 Smyth first baptized himself and then baptized the others.<sup id="cite_ref-Briggs_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1609, while still there, Smyth wrote a tract titled "The Character of the Beast," or "The False Constitution of the Church." In it he expressed two propositions: first, infants are not to be baptized; and second, "Antichristians converted are to be admitted into the true Church by baptism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard200324_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard200324-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Hence, his conviction was that a scriptural church should consist only of regenerate believers who have been baptized on a personal confession of faith. He rejected the Separatist movement's doctrine of infant baptism (<a href="/wiki/Paedobaptism" class="mw-redirect" title="Paedobaptism">paedobaptism</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, Smyth left the group, and layman Thomas Helwys took over the leadership, leading the church back to England in 1611.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, Smyth became committed to believers' baptism as the only biblical baptism. He was convinced on the basis of his interpretation of Scripture that infants would not be damned should they die in infancy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard200325_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard200325-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Smyth, convinced that his self-baptism was invalid, applied with the <a href="/wiki/Mennonites" title="Mennonites">Mennonites</a> for membership. He died while waiting for membership, and some of his followers became Mennonites. Thomas Helwys and others kept their baptism and their Baptist commitments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard200325_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard200325-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>  The modern Baptist denomination is an outgrowth of Smyth's movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Briggs_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Baptists rejected the name Anabaptist when they were called that by opponents in derision. McBeth writes that as late as the 18th century, many Baptists referred to themselves as "the Christians commonly—though <i>falsely</i>—called Anabaptists."<sup id="cite_ref-McBeth_Bapt_Beg_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McBeth_Bapt_Beg-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another milestone in the early development of Baptist doctrine was in 1638 with <a href="/wiki/John_Spilsbury_(Baptist_minister)" title="John Spilsbury (Baptist minister)">John Spilsbury</a>, a Calvinistic minister who helped to promote the strict practice of believer's <a href="/wiki/Immersion_baptism" title="Immersion baptism">baptism by immersion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ETS_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETS-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>  According to Tom Nettles, professor of historical theology at <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Theological_Seminary" title="Southern Baptist Theological Seminary">Southern Baptist Theological Seminary</a>, "Spilsbury's cogent arguments for a gathered, disciplined congregation of believers baptized by immersion as constituting the New Testament church gave expression to and built on insights that had emerged within separatism, advanced in the life of John Smyth and the suffering congregation of Thomas Helwys, and matured in Particular Baptists."<sup id="cite_ref-ETS_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETS-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anabaptist_influence_view">Anabaptist influence view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Anabaptist influence view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TheDippersDipt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TheDippersDipt.jpg/220px-TheDippersDipt.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TheDippersDipt.jpg/330px-TheDippersDipt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TheDippersDipt.jpg/440px-TheDippersDipt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2060" data-file-height="2891" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TheDippersDipt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Print from Anglican theologian <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Featley" title="Daniel Featley">Daniel Featley</a>'s book, "The Dippers Dipt, or, The Anabaptists Duck'd and Plung'd Over Head and Ears, at a Disputation in Southwark", published in 1645.</div></div></div>
<p>A minority view is that early-17th-century Baptists were influenced by (but not directly connected to) continental <a href="/wiki/Anabaptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Priest_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Priest-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> According to this view, the General Baptists shared similarities with Dutch Waterlander Mennonites (one of many Anabaptist groups) including believer's baptism only, religious liberty, separation of church and state, and <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminian</a> views of salvation, predestination and original sin. Representative writers including A.C. Underwood and William R. Estep. Gourley wrote that among some contemporary Baptist scholars who emphasize the faith of the community over soul liberty, the Anabaptist influence theory is making a comeback.<sup id="cite_ref-Gourley_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gourley-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the relations between Baptists and Anabaptists were early strained. In 1624, the then five existing Baptist churches of London issued a condemnation of the Anabaptists.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the original group associated with Smyth and popularly believed to be the first Baptists broke with the Waterlander Mennonite Anabaptists after a brief period of association in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perpetuity_and_succession_view">Perpetuity and succession view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Perpetuity and succession view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baptist_successionism" title="Baptist successionism">Baptist successionism</a></div>
<p>Traditional Baptist historians write from the perspective that Baptists had existed since the time of Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbet197518–9_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbet197518–9-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> passed resolutions rejecting this view in 1859.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Proponents of the Baptist successionist or perpetuity view consider the Baptist movement to have existed independently from <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholicism</a> and prior to the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-H._Leon_McBeth_pages_59-60_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H._Leon_McBeth_pages_59-60-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The perpetuity view is often identified with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Trail_of_Blood" title="The Trail of Blood">The Trail of Blood</a></i>, a booklet of five lectures by J.M. Carrol published in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-H._Leon_McBeth_pages_59-60_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H._Leon_McBeth_pages_59-60-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  Other Baptist writers who advocate the successionist theory of Baptist origins are <a href="/wiki/John_T._Christian" title="John T. Christian">John T. Christian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Crosby_(Baptist)" title="Thomas Crosby (Baptist)">Thomas Crosby</a>, G. H. Orchard, J. M. Cramp, William Cathcart, Adam Taylor and D. B. Ray<sup id="cite_ref-H._Leon_McBeth_pages_59-60_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H._Leon_McBeth_pages_59-60-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbet197518_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbet197518-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  This view was also held by English Baptist preacher, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Spurgeon" title="Charles Spurgeon">Charles Spurgeon</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Mercer" title="Jesse Mercer">Jesse Mercer</a>, the namesake of Mercer University.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1898 William Whitsitt was pressured to resign his presidency of the Southern Baptist Theological Seminary for denying Baptist successionism <sup id="cite_ref-James_H._Slatton_pages_278-293_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_H._Slatton_pages_278-293-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptist_origins_in_the_United_Kingdom">Baptist origins in the United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Baptist origins in the United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">English Dissenters</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Title_Page_A_Short_Declaration_of_the_Mistery_of_Iniquity.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Title_Page_A_Short_Declaration_of_the_Mistery_of_Iniquity.jpg/220px-Title_Page_A_Short_Declaration_of_the_Mistery_of_Iniquity.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Title_Page_A_Short_Declaration_of_the_Mistery_of_Iniquity.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="459" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Title_Page_A_Short_Declaration_of_the_Mistery_of_Iniquity.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A Short Declaration of the Mistery of Iniquity</i> (1612) by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helwys" title="Thomas Helwys">Thomas Helwys</a>. For Helwys, religious liberty was a right for everyone, even for those he disagreed with.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1612, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Helwys" title="Thomas Helwys">Thomas Helwys</a> established a Baptist congregation in London, consisting of congregants from Smyth's church. A number of other Baptist churches sprang up, and they became known as the General Baptists. The Particular Baptists were established when a group of Calvinist Separatists adopted believers' Baptism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright2004_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright2004-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2014)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Particular Baptists consisted of seven churches by 1644 and had created a confession of faith called the First London Confession of Faith.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptist_origins_in_North_America">Baptist origins in North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Baptist origins in North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baptists_in_the_United_States" title="Baptists in the United States">Baptists in the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baptists_in_Canada" title="Baptists in Canada">Baptists in Canada</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saddleback_church_Lake_Forest_building2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Saddleback_church_Lake_Forest_building2.jpg/280px-Saddleback_church_Lake_Forest_building2.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Saddleback_church_Lake_Forest_building2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="213" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saddleback_church_Lake_Forest_building2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Building of <a href="/wiki/Saddleback_Church" title="Saddleback Church">Saddleback Church</a>, affiliated to the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a>, in 2016, in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Forest,_California" title="Lake Forest, California">Lake Forest, California</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a></div></div></div>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Roger_Williams" title="Roger Williams">Roger Williams</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Clarke_(Baptist_minister)" title="John Clarke (Baptist minister)">John Clarke</a>, his compatriot and coworker for religious freedom, are variously credited as founding the earliest Baptist church in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-Newport_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newport-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 1639, Williams established a Baptist church in <a href="/wiki/Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="Providence, Rhode Island">Providence, Rhode Island</a>, and Clarke began a Baptist church in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a>. According to a Baptist historian who has researched the matter extensively, "There is much debate over the centuries as to whether the Providence or Newport church deserved the place of 'first' Baptist congregation in America. Exact records for both congregations are lacking."<sup id="cite_ref-Brackney_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brackney-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Awakening" title="Great Awakening">Great Awakening</a> energized the Baptist movement, and the Baptist community experienced spectacular growth. Baptists became the largest Christian community in many southern states, including among the black population.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Baptist missionary work in Canada began in the British colony of <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> (present day Nova Scotia and <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>) in the 1760s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBumstead198440_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBumstead198440-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The first official record of a Baptist church in Canada was that of the Horton Baptist Church (now Wolfville) in Wolfville, Nova Scotia on 29 October 1778.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBumstead198462_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBumstead198462-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>  The church was established with the assistance of the <a href="/wiki/New_Light" class="mw-redirect" title="New Light">New Light</a> evangelist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Alline" title="Henry Alline">Henry Alline</a>. Many of Alline's followers, after his death, would convert and strengthen the Baptist presence in the Atlantic region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBumstead1984_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBumstead1984-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (June 2014)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rawlyk_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rawlyk-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  Two major groups of Baptists formed the basis of the churches in the Maritimes. These were referred to as Regular Baptist (Calvinistic in their doctrine) and Free Will Baptists (Arminian in their doctrine).<sup id="cite_ref-Rawlyk_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rawlyk-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1845, the Baptist congregations in the United States split over slavery and missions. The <a href="/wiki/American_Baptist_Home_Mission_Society" title="American Baptist Home Mission Society">Home Mission Society</a> prevented slaveholders from being appointed as missionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Early_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Early-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The split created the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a>, while the northern congregations formed their own umbrella organization now called the <a href="/wiki/American_Baptist_Churches_USA" title="American Baptist Churches USA">American Baptist Churches USA</a> (ABC-USA). The <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Episcopal_Church,_South" title="Methodist Episcopal Church, South">Methodist Episcopal Church, South</a> had recently separated over the issue of slavery, and southern <a href="/wiki/Presbyterians" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterians">Presbyterians</a> would do so shortly thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-Southern_Baptist_Beginnings_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Southern_Baptist_Beginnings-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptist_origins_in_Ukraine">Baptist origins in Ukraine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Baptist origins in Ukraine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baptists_in_Ukraine" title="Baptists in Ukraine">Baptists in Ukraine</a></div>
<p>The Baptist churches in Ukraine were preceded by the German Anabaptist and Mennonite communities, who had been living in the South of Ukraine since the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The first Baptist <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> (adult baptism by full immersion) in Ukraine took place in 1864 on the river <a href="/wiki/Inhul" title="Inhul">Inhul</a> in the Yelizavetgrad region (now <a href="/wiki/Kropyvnytskyi" title="Kropyvnytskyi">Kropyvnytskyi</a> region), in a <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germans_in_Russia,_Ukraine_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="History of Germans in Russia, Ukraine and the Soviet Union">German settlement</a>. In 1867, the first Baptist communities were organized in that area. From there, the Baptist movement spread across the south of Ukraine and then to other regions as well. One of the first Baptist communities was registered in <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a> in 1907, and in 1908 the First All-<a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a> Convention of Baptists was held there, as Ukraine was still controlled by the Russian Empire. The All-Russian Union of Baptists was established in the town of Yekaterinoslav (now <a href="/wiki/Dnipro" title="Dnipro">Dnipro</a>) in Southern Ukraine. At the end of the 19th century, estimates are that there were from 100,000 to 300,000 Baptists in Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> An independent All-Ukrainian Baptist Union of Ukraine was established during the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_People%27s_Republic" title="Ukrainian People&#39;s Republic">brief period of Ukraine's independence</a> in early 20th-century, and once again after the fall of the Soviet Union, the largest of which is currently known as the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Baptist_Union_of_Ukraine" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Baptist Union of Ukraine">Evangelical Baptist Union of Ukraine</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptist_affiliations">Baptist affiliations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Baptist affiliations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
