<p>Many Baptist churches choose to affiliate with organizational groups that provide fellowship without control.<sup id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The largest such group in the US is the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a>. There also are a substantial number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Baptist_sub-denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Baptist sub-denominations">smaller cooperative groups</a>. Finally, there are <a href="/wiki/Independent_Baptist" title="Independent Baptist">Independent Baptist</a> churches that choose to remain independent of any denomination, organization, or association.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that a primary Baptist principle is that local Baptist Churches are independent and self-governing,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and if so the term 'Baptist denomination' may be considered somewhat incongruous.
</p><p>In 1905, Baptists worldwide formed the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_World_Alliance" title="Baptist World Alliance">Baptist World Alliance</a> (BWA).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The BWA's goals include caring for the needy, leading in world evangelism and defending human rights and religious freedom. Though it played a role in the founding of the BWA, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> severed its affiliation with BWA in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Baptist_Meetinghouse,_Providence,_RI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/First_Baptist_Meetinghouse%2C_Providence%2C_RI.jpg/200px-First_Baptist_Meetinghouse%2C_Providence%2C_RI.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/First_Baptist_Meetinghouse%2C_Providence%2C_RI.jpg/300px-First_Baptist_Meetinghouse%2C_Providence%2C_RI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/First_Baptist_Meetinghouse%2C_Providence%2C_RI.jpg/400px-First_Baptist_Meetinghouse%2C_Providence%2C_RI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1047" data-file-height="1821" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Baptist_Meetinghouse,_Providence,_RI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/First_Baptist_Church_in_America" title="First Baptist Church in America">First Baptist Church in America</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="Providence, Rhode Island">Providence, Rhode Island</a>. <a href="/wiki/Baptists_in_the_United_States" title="Baptists in the United States">Baptists in the U.S.</a> number 50 million people and constitute roughly one-third of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_the_United_States" title="Protestantism in the United States">American Protestants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations_by_number_of_members" title="List of Christian denominations by number of members">List of Christian denominations by number of members</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Baptist_denominations" title="List of Baptist denominations">List of Baptist denominations</a></div>
<p>In 2010, 100 million Christians identify themselves as Baptist or belong to Baptist-type churches.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_World_Alliance" title="Baptist World Alliance">Baptist World Alliance</a> has 47 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-BWA_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWA-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>  Many Baptist groups, including the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_Bible_Fellowship" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptist Bible Fellowship">Baptist Bible Fellowship</a> do not cooperate with the Alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Baptists are present in almost all continents in large denominations. The largest communities that are part of the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_World_Alliance" title="Baptist World Alliance">Baptist World Alliance</a> are in Nigeria (3.5 million) and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> (2 million) in Africa, India (2.5 million) and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> (1 million) in Asia, the United States (35 million) and Brazil (1.8 million) in the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> had the second largest Baptist community in the world, behind only the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-NCEEER_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCEEER-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Barna Group researchers, Baptists are the largest denominational grouping of <a href="/wiki/Born_again_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Born again (Christianity)">born again Christians</a> in the USA.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  A 2009 ABCNEWS/Beliefnet phone poll of 1,022 adults suggests that fifteen percent of Americans identify themselves as Baptists.<sup id="cite_ref-abcnews.go.com_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews.go.com-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A large percentage of Baptists in North America are found in six bodies—the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> (SBC); <a href="/wiki/American_Baptist_Association" title="American Baptist Association">American Baptist Association</a> (ABA); <a href="/wiki/National_Baptist_Convention,_USA,_Inc." title="National Baptist Convention, USA, Inc.">National Baptist Convention</a> (NBC); <a href="/wiki/National_Baptist_Convention_of_America,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="National Baptist Convention of America, Inc.">National Baptist Convention of America, Inc.</a>; (NBCA); <a href="/wiki/American_Baptist_Churches_USA" title="American Baptist Churches USA">American Baptist Churches USA</a> (ABC); and <a href="/wiki/Baptist_Bible_Fellowship_International" title="Baptist Bible Fellowship International">Baptist Bible Fellowship International</a> (BBFI).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are three states in the world with a Baptist majority: <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> in USA (over 50&#160;%), and <a href="/wiki/Nagaland" title="Nagaland">Nagaland</a> in India (more than 75&#160;%).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualification_for_membership">Qualification for membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Qualification for membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Membership policies vary due to the autonomy of churches, but the traditional method by which an individual becomes a member of a church is through <a href="/wiki/Believer%27s_baptism" title="Believer&#39;s baptism">believer's baptism</a> (which is a public profession of faith in Jesus, followed by water baptism).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most baptists do not believe that baptism is a requirement for salvation, but rather a public expression of one's inner repentance and faith.<sup id="cite_ref-Brackney_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brackney-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  Therefore, some churches will admit into membership persons who make a profession without believer's baptism.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, Baptist churches do not have a stated age restriction on membership, but believer's baptism requires that an individual be able to freely and earnestly profess their faith.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>  (See <a href="/wiki/Believer%27s_Baptism#Age_of_accountability" class="mw-redirect" title="Believer&#39;s Baptism">Age of Accountability</a>)
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptist_beliefs_and_principles">Baptist beliefs and principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Baptist beliefs and principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baptist_beliefs" title="Baptist beliefs">Baptist beliefs</a></div>
<p>Baptists, like other Christians, are defined by school of thought—some of it common to all orthodox and evangelical groups and a portion of it distinctive to Baptists.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Through the years, different Baptist groups have issued confessions of faith—without considering them to be <i>creeds</i>—to express their particular doctrinal distinctions in comparison to other Christians as well as in comparison to other Baptists.<sup id="cite_ref-Shurden_fragile_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shurden_fragile-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Baptist denominations are traditionally seen as belonging to two parties, <a href="/wiki/General_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="General Baptist">General Baptists</a> who uphold <a href="/wiki/Arminian" class="mw-redirect" title="Arminian">Arminian</a> theology and <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Baptists" title="Reformed Baptists">Particular Baptists</a> who uphold <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> theology.<sup id="cite_ref-Benedict1848_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benedict1848-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Holiness_movement" title="Holiness movement">holiness movement</a>, some General Baptists accepted the teaching of a <a href="/wiki/Second_work_of_grace" title="Second work of grace">second work of grace</a> and formed denominations that emphasized this belief, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Valley_Association_of_the_Christian_Baptist_Churches_of_God" title="Ohio Valley Association of the Christian Baptist Churches of God">Ohio Valley Association of the Christian Baptist Churches of God</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holiness_Baptist_Association" title="Holiness Baptist Association">Holiness Baptist Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2002_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2002-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Most Baptists are evangelical in doctrine, but Baptist beliefs can vary due to the congregational governance system that gives autonomy to individual local Baptist churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Baptist_Origins_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baptist_Origins-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Historically, Baptists have played a key role in encouraging religious freedom and separation of church and state.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross,_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_(CVN_70),_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross%2C_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29%2C_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg/220px-US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross%2C_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29%2C_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross%2C_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29%2C_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg/330px-US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross%2C_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29%2C_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross%2C_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29%2C_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg/440px-US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross%2C_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN_70%29%2C_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1976" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_110508-N-DR144-024_Lt._Jeffrey_Ross,_a_chaplain_aboard_the_Nimitz-class_aircraft_carrier_USS_Carl_Vinson_(CVN_70),_baptizes_Aviation_Boatsw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Baptist chaplain aboard a US navy aircraft carrier baptizes a <a href="/wiki/Mate_(naval_officer)" title="Mate (naval officer)">mate</a></div></div></div>
<p>Shared doctrines would include beliefs about one God; the virgin birth; miracles; atonement for sins through the death, burial, and bodily resurrection of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>; the need for salvation (through belief in Jesus Christ as the Son of God, his death and resurrection); grace; the Kingdom of God; last things (eschatology) (Jesus Christ will return personally and visibly in glory to the earth, the dead will be raised, and Christ will judge everyone in righteousness); and evangelism and missions. Some historically significant Baptist doctrinal documents include the <a href="/wiki/1689_London_Baptist_Confession_of_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="1689 London Baptist Confession of Faith">1689 London Baptist Confession of Faith</a>, 1742 Philadelphia Baptist Confession, the <a href="/wiki/1833_New_Hampshire_Baptist_Confession_of_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="1833 New Hampshire Baptist Confession of Faith">1833 New Hampshire Baptist Confession of Faith</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Baptist_Faith_and_Message" title="Baptist Faith and Message">Baptist Faith and Message</a>,</i> and written <a href="/wiki/Church_covenant" title="Church covenant">church covenants</a> which some individual Baptist churches adopt as a statement of their faith and beliefs.
</p><p>Most Baptists hold that no church or ecclesiastical organization has inherent authority over a Baptist church. Churches can properly relate to each other under this polity only through voluntary cooperation, never by any sort of coercion. Furthermore, this Baptist polity calls for freedom from governmental control.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Exceptions to this local form of local governance include a few churches that submit to the leadership of a body of <a href="/wiki/Elder_(Christianity)#Baptists" title="Elder (Christianity)">elders</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopal Baptist">Episcopal Baptists</a> that have an <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">Episcopal system</a>.
</p><p>Baptists generally believe in the literal <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> of Christ. Beliefs among Baptists regarding the "<a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">end times</a>" include <a href="/wiki/Amillennialism" title="Amillennialism">amillennialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dispensationalism" title="Dispensationalism">dispensationalism</a>, and historic <a href="/wiki/Premillennialism" title="Premillennialism">premillennialism</a>, with views such as <a href="/wiki/Postmillennialism" title="Postmillennialism">postmillennialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Preterism" title="Preterism">preterism</a> receiving some support.
</p><p>Some additional distinctive Baptist principles held by many Baptists:<sup id="cite_ref-Newman_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The supremacy of the canonical Scriptures as a norm of faith and practice. For something to become a matter of faith and practice, it is not sufficient for it to be merely <i>consistent with</i> and not contrary to scriptural principles. It must be something <i>explicitly</i> ordained through command or example in the Bible. For instance, this is why Baptists do not practice infant baptism—they say the Bible neither commands nor exemplifies infant baptism as a Christian practice. More than any other Baptist principle, this one when applied to infant baptism is said to separate Baptists from other evangelical Christians.</li>
<li>Baptists believe that faith is a matter between God and the individual (<a href="/wiki/Religious_freedom" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious freedom">religious freedom</a>). To them it means the advocacy of absolute liberty of conscience.</li>
<li>Insistence on immersion as the only mode of baptism. Baptists do not believe that baptism is necessary for salvation. Therefore, for Baptists, baptism is an <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_(Christianity)" title="Ordinance (Christianity)">ordinance</a>, not a sacrament, since, in their view, it imparts no saving grace.<sup id="cite_ref-Newman_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_that_vary_among_Baptists">Beliefs that vary among Baptists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Beliefs that vary among Baptists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg/220px-KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg/330px-KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg/440px-KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="813" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KJV_1611_Rice_Baptist_Church_New_Market_Alabama_2012-06-13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Church sign indicating that the congregation uses the Authorized King James Version of 1611</div></div></div>
<p>Since there is no hierarchical authority and each Baptist church is autonomous, there is no official set of Baptist theological beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> These differences exist both among associations, and even among churches within the associations.
</p><p>Some doctrinal issues on which there is widespread difference among Baptists are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">Eschatology</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminianism</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> (<a href="/wiki/General_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="General Baptist">General Baptists</a> uphold Arminian theology while <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Baptists" title="Reformed Baptists">Particular Baptists</a> teach Reformed theology).<sup id="cite_ref-Benedict1848_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benedict1848-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_separation" title="Doctrine of separation">doctrine of separation</a> from "the world" and whether to associate with those who are "of the world"</li>
<li>Belief in a <a href="/wiki/Second_work_of_grace" title="Second work of grace">second work of grace</a>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Entire_sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Entire sanctification">entire sanctification</a> (held by General Baptists in the Holiness tradition)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glossolalia" title="Glossolalia">Speaking-in-tongues</a> and the operation of other <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_gift" title="Spiritual gift">charismatic gifts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> in the modern church<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>How the Bible should be interpreted (<a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a>)</li>
<li>The extent to which <a href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission (Christian)">missionary</a> boards should be used to support missionaries</li>
<li>The extent to which non-members may participate in the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Lord's Supper</a> services</li>
<li>Which translation of Scripture to use (see <a href="/wiki/King-James-Only_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="King-James-Only movement">King-James-Only movement</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dispensationalism" title="Dispensationalism">Dispensationalism</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Covenant_theology" title="Covenant theology">Covenant theology</a></li>
<li>The role of <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_of_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian views of marriage">women in marriage</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women_in_Protestant_denominations#Baptist" title="Ordination of women in Protestant denominations">ordination of women</a> as deacons or pastors.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Attitudes to, and involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical movement">ecumenical movement</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_that_have_shaped_Baptists">Controversies that have shaped Baptists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Controversies that have shaped Baptists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Baptists have faced many controversies in their 400-year history, controversies of the level of crises. Baptist historian Walter Shurden says the word "crisis" comes from the Greek word meaning "to decide." Shurden writes that contrary to the presumed negative view of crises, some controversies that reach a crisis level may actually be "positive and highly productive." He claims that even schism, though never ideal, has often produced positive results. In his opinion crises among Baptists each have become decision-moments that shaped their future.<sup id="cite_ref-Shurden_crises_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shurden_crises-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Some controversies that have shaped Baptists include the "missions crisis", the "slavery crisis", the "landmark crisis", and the "modernist crisis".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Missions_crisis">Missions crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Missions crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early in the 19th century, the rise of the modern <a href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission (Christian)">missions</a> movement, and the backlash against it, led to widespread and bitter controversy among the American Baptists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian1926404–20_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian1926404–20-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>  During this era, the American Baptists were split between missionary and anti-missionary. A substantial secession of Baptists went into the movement led by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Campbell_(clergyman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Campbell (clergyman)">Alexander Campbell</a>, to return to a more fundamental church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristian1926421–36_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristian1926421–36-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_crisis">Slavery crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Slavery crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_slavery" title="Christian views on slavery">Christian views on slavery</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Baptist_Church,_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/First_Baptist_Church%2C_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg/200px-First_Baptist_Church%2C_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/First_Baptist_Church%2C_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg/300px-First_Baptist_Church%2C_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/First_Baptist_Church%2C_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg/400px-First_Baptist_Church%2C_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5666" data-file-height="3778" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:First_Baptist_Church,_Augusta_GA_20160703_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Baptist_Church_(Augusta,_Georgia)" title="First Baptist Church (Augusta, Georgia)">First Baptist Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Augusta,_Georgia" title="Augusta, Georgia">Augusta, Georgia</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> was founded</div></div></div>
<p>Leading up to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Baptists became embroiled in the controversy over <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery in the United States</a>. Whereas in the First <a href="/wiki/Great_Awakening" title="Great Awakening">Great Awakening</a> <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> and Baptist preachers had opposed slavery and urged manumission, over the decades they made more of an accommodation with the institution. They worked with slaveholders in the South to urge a paternalistic institution. Both denominations made direct appeals to slaves and free blacks for conversion. The Baptists particularly allowed them active roles in congregations. By the mid-19th century, northern Baptists tended to oppose slavery. As tensions increased, in 1844 the <a href="/wiki/American_Baptist_Home_Mission_Society" title="American Baptist Home Mission Society">Home Mission Society</a> refused to appoint a slaveholder as a missionary who had been proposed by Georgia. It noted that missionaries could not take servants with them, and also that the board did not want to appear to condone slavery.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> was formed by nine state conventions in 1845. They believed that the Bible sanctions slavery and that it was acceptable for Christians to own slaves. They believed slavery was a human institution which Baptist teaching could make less harsh. By this time many planters were part of Baptist congregations, and some of the denomination's prominent preachers, such as the Rev. <a href="/wiki/Basil_Manly,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Basil Manly, Sr.">Basil Manly, Sr.</a>, president of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a>, were also <a href="/wiki/Plantations_in_the_American_South" title="Plantations in the American South">planters</a> who owned slaves.
</p><p>As early as the late 18th century, black Baptists began to organize separate churches, associations and mission agencies. Blacks set up some independent Baptist congregations in the South before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. White Baptist associations maintained some oversight of these churches.
</p><p>In the postwar years, <a href="/wiki/Freedmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedmen">freedmen</a> quickly left the white congregations and associations, setting up their own churches.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In 1866 the Consolidated American Baptist Convention, formed from black Baptists of the South and West, helped southern associations set up black state conventions, which they did in <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>. In 1880 black state conventions united in the national Foreign Mission Convention, to support black Baptist missionary work. Two other national black conventions were formed, and in 1895 they united as the <a href="/wiki/National_Baptist_Convention,_USA,_Inc." title="National Baptist Convention, USA, Inc.">National Baptist Convention</a>. This organization later went through its own changes, spinning off other conventions. It is the largest black religious organization and the second-largest Baptist organization in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  Baptists are numerically most dominant in the Southeast.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>'s Religious Landscape Survey found that 45% of all African Americans identify with Baptist denominations, with the vast majority of those being within the historically black tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean_islands">Caribbean islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Caribbean islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A healthy Church kills error, and tears evil in pieces! Not so very long ago our nation tolerated slavery in our colonies. Philanthropists endeavored to destroy slavery, but when was it utterly abolished? It was when <a href="/wiki/William_Wilberforce" title="William Wilberforce">Wilberforce</a> roused the Church of God, and when the Church of God addressed herself to the conflict—then she tore the evil thing to pieces! -- <a href="/wiki/Charles_Spurgeon" title="Charles Spurgeon">C.H. Spurgeon</a> an outspoken British Baptist opponent of slavery in 'The Best War Cry' (1883)<sup id="cite_ref-CHS_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CHS-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Elsewhere in the Americas, in the Caribbean in particular, Baptist missionaries and members took an active role in the anti-slavery movement. In Jamaica, for example, <a href="/wiki/William_Knibb" title="William Knibb">William Knibb</a>, a prominent British Baptist missionary, worked toward the emancipation of slaves in the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a> (which took place in full in 1838). Knibb also supported the creation of "<a href="/wiki/Free_Villages" title="Free Villages">Free Villages</a>" and sought funding from English Baptists to buy land for freedmen to cultivate; the Free Villages were envisioned as rural communities to be centred around a Baptist church where emancipated slaves could farm their own land. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burchell" title="Thomas Burchell">Thomas Burchell</a>, missionary minister in <a href="/wiki/Montego_Bay" title="Montego Bay">Montego Bay</a>, also was active in this movement, gaining funds from Baptists in England to buy land for what became known as Burchell Free Village.
</p><p>Prior to emancipation, Baptist deacon <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sharpe" title="Samuel Sharpe">Samuel Sharpe</a>, who served with Burchell, organized a general strike of slaves seeking better conditions. It developed into a major rebellion of as many as 60,000 slaves, which became known as the Christmas Rebellion (when it took place) or the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_War" title="Baptist War">Baptist War</a>. It was put down by government troops within two weeks. During and after the rebellion, an estimated 200 slaves were killed outright, with more than 300 judicially executed later by prosecution in the courts, sometimes for minor offenses.
</p><p>Baptists were active after emancipation in promoting the education of former slaves; for example, Jamaica's <a href="/wiki/Calabar_High_School" title="Calabar High School">Calabar High School</a>, named after the port of <a href="/wiki/Calabar" title="Calabar">Calabar</a> in Nigeria, was founded by Baptist missionaries. At the same time, during and after slavery, slaves and free blacks formed their own <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_Baptist" title="Spiritual Baptist">Spiritual Baptist</a> movements - breakaway spiritual movements which theology often expressed resistance to oppression.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory_of_slavery">Memory of slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Memory of slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington,_D.C._(Dr._Martin_Luther_King,_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.)_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg/220px-Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg/330px-Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg/440px-Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1974" data-file-height="1565" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington,_D.C._(Dr._Martin_Luther_King,_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.)_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> at the 1963 civil rights march on Washington, D.C.</div></div></div>
<p>In the American South, the interpretation of the American Civil War, abolition of slavery and postwar period has differed sharply by race since those years. Americans have often interpreted great events in religious terms. Historian Wilson Fallin contrasts the interpretation of Civil War and Reconstruction in white versus black memory by analyzing Baptist sermons documented in Alabama. Soon after the Civil War, most black Baptists in the South left the Southern Baptist Convention, reducing its numbers by hundreds of thousands or more.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> They quickly organized their own congregations and developed their own regional and state associations and, by the end of the 19th century, a national convention.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>White preachers in Alabama after Reconstruction expressed the view that:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>God had chastised them and given them a special mission – to maintain orthodoxy, strict biblicism, personal piety, and "traditional" race relations. Slavery, they insisted, had not been sinful. Rather, emancipation was a historical tragedy and the end of Reconstruction was a clear sign of God's favor.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Black preachers interpreted the Civil War, Emancipation and Reconstruction as:
"God's gift of freedom." They had a gospel of liberation, having long identified with the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a> from slavery in the Old Testament. They took opportunities to exercise their independence, to worship in their own way, to affirm their worth and dignity, and to proclaim the fatherhood of God and the brotherhood of man. Most of all, they quickly formed their own churches, associations, and conventions to operate freely without white supervision. These institutions offered self-help and racial uplift, a place to develop and use leadership, and places for proclamation of the gospel of liberation. As a result, black preachers said that God would protect and help him and God's people; God would be their rock in a stormy land.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Southern Baptist Convention supported white supremacy and its results: <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era">disenfranchising most blacks and many poor whites</a> at the turn of the 20th century by raising barriers to voter registration, and passage of <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> laws that enforced the system of <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" class="mw-redirect" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Its members largely resisted the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> in the South, which sought to enforce their constitutional rights for public access and voting; and enforcement of midcentury federal civil rights laws.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>On 20 June 1995, the Southern Baptist Convention voted to adopt a resolution renouncing its racist roots and apologizing for its past defense of slavery. More than 20,000 Southern Baptists registered for the meeting in Atlanta. The resolution declared that messengers, as SBC delegates are called, "unwaveringly denounce racism, in all its forms, as deplorable sin" and "lament and repudiate historic acts of evil such as slavery from which we continue to reap a bitter harvest." It offered an apology to all African Americans for "condoning and/or perpetuating individual and systemic racism in our lifetime" and repentance for "racism of which we have been guilty, whether consciously or unconsciously."  Although Southern Baptists have condemned racism in the past, this was the first time the convention, predominantly white since the Reconstruction era, had specifically addressed the issue of slavery.
</p><p>The statement sought forgiveness "from our African-American brothers and sisters" and pledged to "eradicate racism in all its forms from Southern Baptist life and ministry." In 1995 about 500,000 members of the 15.6-million-member denomination were African Americans and another 300,000 were <a href="/wiki/Ethnic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic">ethnic</a> minorities. The resolution marked the denomination's first formal acknowledgment that <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> played a role in its founding.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmark_crisis">Landmark crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Landmark crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Landmarkism" title="Landmarkism">Landmarkism</a></div>
<p>Southern Baptist <a href="/wiki/Landmarkism" title="Landmarkism">Landmarkism</a> sought to reset the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_separation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical separation">ecclesiastical separation</a> which had characterized the old Baptist churches, in an era when inter-denominational union meetings were the order of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Robinson_Graves" title="James Robinson Graves">James Robinson Graves</a> was an influential Baptist of the 19th century and the primary leader of this movement.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> While some Landmarkers eventually separated from the Southern Baptist Convention, the movement continued to influence the Convention into the 20th and 21st centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> For instance, in 2005, the Southern Baptist International Mission Board forbade its missionaries to receive alien immersions for baptism.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernist_crisis">Modernist crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Modernist crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist-Modernist_Controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamentalist-Modernist Controversy">Fundamentalist-Modernist Controversy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spurgeon.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Spurgeon.png/120px-Spurgeon.png" decoding="async" width="120" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Spurgeon.png/180px-Spurgeon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Spurgeon.png 2x" data-file-width="184" data-file-height="357" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spurgeon.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Spurgeon" title="Charles Spurgeon">Charles Spurgeon</a> later in life.</div></div></div>
<p>The rise of <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist%E2%80%93Modernist_Controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamentalist–Modernist Controversy">theological modernism</a> in the latter 19th and early 20th centuries also greatly affected Baptists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbet1975424–45_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbet1975424–45-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>  The Landmark movement, already mentioned, has been described as a reaction among Southern Baptists in the United States against incipient modernism .<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In England, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Haddon_Spurgeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Haddon Spurgeon">Charles Haddon Spurgeon</a> fought against modernistic views of the Scripture in the <a href="/wiki/Downgrade_Controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Downgrade Controversy">Downgrade Controversy</a> and severed his church from the Baptist Union as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbet1975114_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbet1975114-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-downgrade_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-downgrade-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nettles_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nettles-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Baptist_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Baptist Convention">Northern Baptist Convention</a> in the United States had internal conflict over modernism in the early 20th century, ultimately embracing it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbet1975395,_436_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbet1975395,_436-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Two new conservative associations of congregations that separated from the Convention were founded as a result: the <a href="/wiki/General_Association_of_Regular_Baptist_Churches" title="General Association of Regular Baptist Churches">General Association of Regular Baptist Churches</a> in 1933 and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Baptist_Association_of_America" title="Conservative Baptist Association of America">Conservative Baptist Association of America</a> in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbet1975395,_436_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbet1975395,_436-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following similar conflicts over modernism, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention_conservative_resurgence" title="Southern Baptist Convention conservative resurgence">the Southern Baptist Convention adhered to conservative theology</a> as its official position.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>  In the late 20th century, Southern Baptists who disagreed with this direction founded two new groups: the liberal <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Baptists" title="Alliance of Baptists">Alliance of Baptists</a> in 1987 and the more moderate <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_Baptist_Fellowship" title="Cooperative Baptist Fellowship">Cooperative Baptist Fellowship</a> in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-BrackneyBNA138_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BrackneyBNA138-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Originally both schisms continued to identify as Southern Baptist, but over time "became permanent new families of Baptists."<sup id="cite_ref-BrackneyBNA138_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BrackneyBNA138-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptists&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>Bebbington, David. <i>Baptists through the Centuries: A History of a Global People</i> (Baylor University Press, 2010) emphasis on the United States and Europe; the last two chapters are on the global context.</li>
<li>Brackney, William H. <i>A Genetic History of Baptist Thought: With Special Reference to Baptists in Britain and North America</i> (Mercer University Press, 2004), focus on confessions of faith, hymns, theologians, and academics.</li>
<li>Brackney, William H. ed., <i>Historical Dictionary of the Baptists</i> (2nd ed. Scarecrow, 2009).</li>
<li>Cathcart, William, ed. <i>The Baptist Encyclopedia</i> (2 vols. 1883). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/baptistencyclope02cathuoft#page/n7/mode/2up">online</a></li>
<li>Gavins, Raymond. <i>The Perils and Prospects of Southern Black Leadership: Gordon Blaine Hancock, 1884–1970.</i> Duke University Press, 1977.</li>
<li>Harrison, Paul M. <i>Authority and Power in the Free Church Tradition: A Social Case Study of the American Baptist Convention</i> Princeton University Press, 1959.</li>
<li>Harvey, Paul. <i>Redeeming the South: Religious Cultures and Racial Identities among Southern Baptists, 1865–1925</i> University of North Carolina Press, 1997.</li>
<li>Heyrman, Christine Leigh. <i>Southern Cross: The Beginnings of the Bible Belt</i> (1997).</li>
<li>Isaac, Rhy. "Evangelical Revolt: The Nature of the Baptists' Challenge to the Traditional Order in Virginia, 1765 to 1775," <i>William and Mary Quarterly,</i> 3d ser., XXXI (July 1974), 345–68.</li>
<li><cite class="citation"><i>Life &amp; Practice in the Early Church: A Documentary Reader</i>, New York University press, 2001, pp.&#160;5–7, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8147-5648-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8147-5648-5"><bdi>978-0-8147-5648-5</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Life+%26+Practice+in+the+Early+Church%3A+A+Documentary+Reader&amp;rft.pages=5-7&amp;rft.pub=New+York+University+press&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8147-5648-5&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABaptists" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/>.</li>

<li>Rawlyk, George. <i>Champions of the Truth: Fundamentalism, Modernism, and the Maritime Baptists</i> (1990), Canada.</li>
<li>Spangler, Jewel L. "Becoming Baptists: Conversion in Colonial and Early National Virginia" <i>Journal of Southern History.</i> Volume: 67. Issue: 2. 2001. pp.&#160;243+</li>
